96 udāharaṇāntaraṃ kim artham? sakalaviparyayasaṅgrahārthaṃ matāntaraniṣedhārthaṃ ca. kecid atra svapnajñānaṃ pramāṇaphalasmṛtyādibhyo 'rthāntaram icchantīty atas tanniṣedhaḥ kriyate. na hi viparyayalakṣaṇena saṅgṛhītasya tato 'rthāntaratvaṃ yuktam. yad api svapnajñānaṃ viparyayādivilakṣaṇam, tad api nāvidyāntaram. kiṃ kāraṇam? yad tāvad anavadhāraṇātmakam, tatsaṃśayāntarbhūtam. samānadharmādiviṣaya(jñāna)kāraṇābhāvān na suptasya saṃśaya iti cet, na, viṣayaśabdena viṣayiṇas tajjñānasyābhidhānāt. tac ca samānadharmādijñānaṃ samyag vā bhavatu bhrāntaṃ vā ity ubhayathāpi sahakārisahitaṃ saṃśayakāraṇatveneṣṭam, kāryadarśanād dhi kāraṇasya sāmarthyam avadhāryata iti. yac cānubhūtārthamātrāvabhāsisvapnajñānam, tatsmaraṇam. yat tu samyaganubhavātmakam, tatpratyakṣādiphalam, yathā sukhādisaṃvedanam iti. evaṃ ca yat svapnajñānaṃ yallakṣaṇena saṅgṛhītaṃ tat tatraivāntarbhūtaṃ draṣṭavyaṃ jāgrajjñānavan na tebhyo 'rthāntaram iti. 181 Commenting on this in the Nyayabhusana Bhasarvajna says that Viparyaya signifies false ascertainment of an object or a conception of something as what it is not; e.g. that cognition of moon is erroneous in which it is apprehended as two. One may ask here : Why is one more instance, viz. cognition of elephant in a dream, given in the Nyayasara ? That is to say, when one instance, viz. the cognition of two moons is sufficient to explain the nature of Viparyaya another illustration becomes redundant. The answer to this is that the purpose behind giving the second instance is to include all varieties of Viparyaya as well as to refute the rival views regarding the classification of avidya (no-knowledge). Some seek to establish that cognition in a dream is altogether different from the cognition resulting from pramana and from memory etc. The second instance is intentionally given in order to <p.205> refute this view. Indeed, it is not proper to regard the cognition in a dream as different from viparyaya since it is comprehended under the definition of Viparyaya. The dream-cognition cannot be a distinct variety of avidya as recognised by the Vaisesika-sutra and Prasastapadabhasya. It may not be Viparyaya, but if it involves doubt it could be classified as Samsaya. It may be urged that in the case of a person who is asleep doubt cannot arise since there is absence of the cause of doubt, viz. the common attributes and the like (Samanadharmat, etc.). The reply to this is that this is not right, for, the word ’Visaya' (object) signifies the Visayin, the knowledge of that object and this can be there in a dream. That knowledge of common attributes may be either right or wrong. In either case, when it is accompanied by auxiliary causes, it is recognised as a cause of doubt. For, the capability of a cause can indeed be ascertained by the perception of its effect. That is to say, as we have sometimes in a dream, the cognition of the nature of doubt, we must admit that the cause of doubt is present even in the dream. Similarly, the dream - cognition in which an object that is experienced in the waking state just becomes manifest can be called memory. And the dream-cognition which is of the nature of right apprehension can be regarded as the result of perception etc; e.g. the cognition of pleasure etc. in a dream. That is to say, pleasure, pain etc. are really experienced in a dream, as the mind is active. So this experience is of the nature of right apprehension. <p.206> Thus like the cognition in the waking state, the dream-cognition can be included under doubt, memory etc. in accordance with the definition that is applicable to it. The varieties of dream cognition correspond to those of the cognition in the waking state. And so in Bhasarvajna's view, dream-cognitions are not different from those in the waking state so far as classification is concerned. Though dream- cognition may as a whole be regarded as a variety of viparyaya, it can be classified as doubt, memory etc; as the case may be, like the cognition in the waking state. It need not be classified as a distinct variety of avidya.  
    182 Apararkadeva, a commentator of the Nyayasara closely follows here the Nyayabhusana. He says that the fact that a dream occurs to the person whose sense-organs have ceased to function and whose mind has slackened its functioning does not establish the dream as different from Viparyaya. This signifies merely the sub-difference between the characteristics of the two, viz. dream and viparyaya. Further, clarifying the difference between dream and waking state he says that the cognition arising due to sleep is dream, while the state other than this is waking.   
97 nimittabhedenāpy anekadhā viparyayo bhidyate, yad yathā — sādṛśyād rajjau sarpa iti, sthāṇau puruṣa ity evamādi jñānam, dravyāntarasaṃsargāc chukle 'pi paṭādau raktādijñānaṃ sphaṭikādau copādhisaṃnidhānamātrād iti, kramabhāviṣv apy āśubhāvād yugapajjñānam, nāvādiyānāt sthireṣv api caladbuddhiḥ, mantrauṣadhādisāmarthyād indrajālādijñānam ity evamādi bāhyanimittapradhānaṃ viparyayajñānaṃ draṣṭavyam iti. athādhyātmikanimittapradhānaṃ — cakṣurādeḥ pittādyabhibhavāc chaṅkhādiṣu pītādijñānam, timiradoṣāt keśābhāve 'pi keśoṇḍukajñānam, ekasmiṃś candrādāv anekatvāvabhāsaś ca, saṃskārātiśayād asaty api yuvatyādyarthe yuvatyādyavabhāsaḥ, asacchāstrābhyāsād aśreyasy anupāyādiṣu ca viparītabuddhiḥ, adṛṣṭasāmarthyād digādimohaḥ, tathā nidrāsahitebhyaḥ saṃskārātiśayādibhyaḥ svapnajñānaṃ veditavyam iti. 183 Moreover, viparyaya can be classified into different varieties in accordance with the different causes also : Erroneous cognition may arise (i) due to similarity, e.g. a cognition of serpent in respect of a rope; cognition of man in respect of a post, etc; (ii) due to the close contact with another substance, e.g. cognition of 'red' even in respect of white cloth; (iii) due to the vicinity of some extraneous condition (Upadhi), e.g. cognition of redness in respect of (white) crystal etc; (iv) due to the quickness of action, e.g. cognition of simultaneity in respect of actions that are in fact successive - the piercing of hundred leaves with a needle; (v) due to the motion of a boat etc., e.g. cognition of movement even in <p.207> regard to things that are stationary; (vi) due to the power of chants, herbs etc. e.g. cognition of magical appearance etc. - these are cases of illusion which are primarily due to external causes. The following are types of illusion in which internal causes are prominent : (i) The cognition of yellowness or the like arises in respect of conch or the like due to the visual organ or the like, being overpowered by bile or the like; (ii) the cognition of hair-like apparitions arises even in the absence of hair due to a defect in the eye viz. timira, partial blindness; and due to a similar cause arises the cognition of two moons in respect of one moon; (iii) the appearance of a damsel arises due to the intensity of impressions even when she is not there; (iv) the perverse conception of what is not a means as a means, and of what is not conducive to spiritual uplift as conducive to it, arises due to the repetitive study of wrong or misguiding texts; (v) erroneous cognition arises in respect of directions etc. due to the efficacy of adrsta (unseen force of past deeds). (vi) So also dream-cognition should be understood to arise due to the intensity of the residual impressions and the like accompanied by sleep. These are the sub-varieties of viparyaya brought about by external and internal causes.  
98 [26,8]
viṣayadvāreṇa tarhi viparyayo vicāryatāṃ (1) kim akhyātir eva? (2) kiṃ vā asatkhyātiḥ? (3) atha prasiddhārthakhyātiḥ? (4) utālaukikārthakhyātiḥ? (5) kiṃ vā smṛtivipramoṣaḥ? (6) utasvid ātmakhyātiḥ? (7) atha sadasattvādyanirvacanīyārthakhyātiḥ? (8) āhosvid viparītakhyātir iti.		 184 Now, we may consider the nature of viparyaya from the point of view of its object. Here Bhasarvajna puts forth different views of rival thinkers regarding the nature of Viparyaya. In the refutation of some of the rival views, he adopts a peculiar pattern of repudiation in which a view is first put forth and then refuted by arguments establishing a second view which is in its turn refuted from the point of view of a third view and so on. After having refuted seven such views regarding erreneous knowledge (Khyatis) he establishes his own view in this regard, which he calls Viparita-Khyati (cognition of reverse object). It may be asked here : What is Viparyaya ? (i) Is it akhyati, i. e. a cognition having nothing external or internal as its object ? (ii) Or is it asatkhyati, i.e. a cognition of something that is non-existent ? (iii) Or is it prasiddhārthakhyāti i.e. a cognition of an object which is well established by pramanas ? (iv) Or is it alaukikarthakhyati, i.e. <p.208> a cognition of something which is extra-ordinary ? (v) Or is it smrti-Vipramosa i.e. an obscuration of memory? (vi) Or is it atmakhyati, i.e. self cognition, cognition of atman or vijnana itself ? (vii) Or is it anirvacaniya-Khyati, i.e. cognition of something which is inexplicable in terms of ‘existent' or 'non-existent' etc. (viii) Or is it Viparita-Khyati, i.e. cognition of a reverse object ?  
99 {akhyātivādas tannirākaraṇaṃ ca}

[26,13]
tatra pramāṇābhāvāt tāvad akhyātir ayuktā, na, pramāṇopapatteḥ. tathā hi — jālāvabhāsini jñāne tāvad na jalasattāvalambanībhūtāsty abhrāntatvaprasaṅgāt, jalābhāvas tu na pratibhāty evāto nālambanam, ata eva marīcayo 'pi nālambanaṃ pratibhāsābhyupagame 'pi tadgrahaṇasyābhrāntatvaprasaṅga iti. toyākāreṇa marīcigrahaṇam ity apy ayuktam, tadanyatvāt, na hi ghaṭākāreṇa tadanyasya paṭāder grahaṇaṃ dṛṣṭam, tasmān nirālambanaṃ jalādiviparyayajñānaṃ svapnaviparyayavad iti.		 185 The akhyativadin propounds his position as follows : It may be contended that the theory of akhyati or cognition having neither external nor internal object as its support (alambana) is not proper since there is no proof for it. But this contention is not tenable. For, it is explicable on the ground of pramana; says the akhyativadin. The cognition in viparyaya is in fact baseless - it has no object (niralambana). For example, in the erroneous cognition in which water is manifested, the existence of (real) water could not become a basis; for, if it were so admitted, there would arise the contingency of such a cognition not being erroneous cognition at all. On the other hand, the negation of water could not become the basis of such a cognition because it is not manifested therein. Hence also the rays of the sun cannot become basis of the erroneous cognition of water, for, if they are conceded as being manifested in the cognition, there would be the contingency of it not being 'error' at all. It is also not proper to say that the rays of the sun are apprehended in the shape of water, because water is an altogether different entity from the rays. We never apprehend cloth etc. as jar which is totally different from cloth etc. Therefore the erroneous cognition of water is in fact baseless, like the erroneous cognition in a dream.  
100 tad etad ayuktam, viśeṣato 'nirdeśaprasaṅgāt. tatra hi svātmarūpaṃ pararūpaṃ vā na kiṃcid api pratibhāti, tatkena viśeṣeṇa jalajñānaṃ rajatajñānam iti ca vyapadiśyeta? bhrāntisuṣuptāvasthayor aviśeṣaprasaṅgaś ca syāt. na hy atra pratibhāsamānārthavyatirekeṇānyo 'sti viśeṣaḥ, pratibhāsamānaś cārthas tajjñānasyālambanam ity ucyate. katham akhyātir eva viparyaya iti. 186 But this view of the akhyativadin is not proper, for, if it be admitted, there would be the contingency that one could not refer to the erroneous cognition as specific - 'illusion of water,' 'illusion of silver' etc. If neither its own shape nor the shape of another is manifested in erroneous cognition, then by what differentia could it be specifically mentioned as 'the (erroneous) cognition of water' 'that of silver' etc.? Moreover, if the erroneous cognition is admitted as baseless, then there would arise the contingency of two <p.209> states viz. the state of illusion and of susupti (sound sleep) being non-different. If erroneous cognition were admitted as baseless, then the states of Susupti and Viparyaya would have to be accepted as non-different. But as a matter of fact both are different. Indeed there is not any distinctive characteristic of such a cognition except the object that is manifested therein. And the object, e.g. water in the cognition of mirage; that is manifested is said to be its basis. Then how could one assert that what is meant by viparyaya is akhyati (i.e. baseless cognition) alone?  
101 {asatkhyātiḥ}

[27,7]
anyas tv āha — satyaṃ bhavatoktaṃ tathāpi sa pratibhāsamāno 'rthaḥ sadrūpo vāsadrūpo vā vicāryamāṇo nāstīty ato 'satkhyātir eveṣṭā. śuktikādiṣv api na śuklādipratibhāso bhrāntaḥ, kiṃ tarhi? rajatapratibhāsaḥ, sa ca rajatākāras tatra nāstīty ato 'satkhyātir eveti.		 187 Another thinker viz. the asatkhyativadin says that it is true that what is manifested in cognition is its basis. However when we examine whether the object manifested in erroneous cognition is an existent one or a non-existent one, we find that it is non-existent. Hence, only the theory of asatkhyati, the erroneous cognition has a non-existent (asat) thing as its basis - could be acceptable. The manifestation of 'white' etc. in respect of nacre etc. is not erroneous; it is the manifestation of silver that is erroneous. In fact that shape of Silver which appears in the cognition does not exist there. Thus what is meant by Viparyaya is asatkhyati - a cognition in which a non-existent object is manifested.  
102 {tatkhaṇḍanaṃ prasiddhārthakhyātinirūpaṇaṃ ca}

[27,12]
tad apy ayuktam ity aparaḥ. kasmāt? asataḥ khapuṣpādivat pratibhāsāsambhavāt. bhrāntivaicitryābhāvaprasaṅgaś ca. na hy asatkhyātivādino 'rthagataṃ jñānagataṃ vā vaicitryam asti, yenānekākārā bhrāntiḥ pratīyeta, tasmāt pramāṇasiddha eva vicitro 'rthaḥ pratibhāti bhrāntāv api. na ca tasya vicāryamāṇasyāsattvam, vicārasya pratītivyatirekeṇānyasyāsambhavāt, pratītivādhitatvāc ca. karatalāder api hi pratibhāsabalenaiva sattvaṃ sidhyati, sa ca pratibhāso 'nyatrāpy asti, yady apy uttarakālaṃ tathā so 'rtho na pratibhāti, tathāpi yadā pratibhāti tadā tāvad asty eva. anyathā vidyudāder api sattvasiddhir na syād iti. tasmāt prasiddhārthakhyātir eva yukteti.		 188 This view of the asatkhyativadin also is not proper, says another thinker, viz. the Prasiddharthakhyativadin. For, it is not possible that a non-existent thing like sky-flower could be manifest in the cognition. Moreover, if this view be admitted, then the <p.210> contingency would arise that there would be no variety in erroneous cognitions. Indeed the proponent of asatkhyati does not admit any diversity as present in the object or as present in the cognition; so error having various shapes cannot possibly be experienced. Hence what are manifested even in erroneous cognitions are diverse objects that are established by pramanas. Such an object need not be regarded as non-existent, when subjected to a critical examination, since critical examination cannot possibly be anything distinct from apprehension. Moreover this argument that what is cognised in error is something non-existent is contradicted by direct apprehension (pratiti). The object cognised could not be said to be non-existent as it is actually cognised as 'existent'. For the existence of palm, etc. also is established on the strength of their manifestation. This manifestation we find elsewhere also, e.g. in the case of silver-cognition (in respect of a nacre). Though that thing is not so manifested later on (when the nacre is perceived), yet it does exist while it appears. If this were not admitted the existence of lightining etc. also could not be proved. It appears for a moment, then disappears, but this does not prove its non-existence. Therefore, the theory of Prasiddharthakhyati alone is proper.  
103 {tatpratyākhyānam alaukikārthakhyātivarṇanaṃ ca}

[27,21]
naitad apy upapannaṃ yathā vyavasthitārthagrahaṇāviśeṣe hi bhrāntābhrāntavyavahārābhāvaḥ syāt. api cottaratrodakāder abhāve 'pi taccihnasya bhūsnigdhatāder upalambhaḥ syāt. na hi vidyudāder ivodakāder apy āśubhāvī niranvayo vināśaḥ kvacid upalabhyate, sarvataddeśadraṣṭṭavisaṃvādenopalambhaś ca vidyudāder iva syāt. vādhyavādhakabhāvaś ca na prāpnoti, sarvajñānānāṃ prasiddhārthaviṣayatvāviśeṣād iti. tasmād alaukiko 'yam artha evaṃ pratibhāti, tenālaukikārthakhyātir eveṣṭety āha kaścit.		 189 The above theory also is not tenable, according to the proponent of alaukikartha-Khyati, the theory of erroneous cognition in which something alaukika, extra-ordinary is manifested. If an <p.211>object is admitted as it is alike in erroneous cognition as well as in valid cognition, then there would not be the usage - 'this is erroneous cognition,' 'this is non-erroneous cognition.' Moreover, if water well-established by pramana were perceived in erroneous cognition, then some sign indicating the previous presence of water, such as viscidity (snigdhata), etc. should be apprehended later on, even when water is not there. It is never found that the destruction of water, etc. also is instantaneous and that it leaves no trace as in the case of lightning. If there were real water in illusion it must be apprehended consistently by all the persons who can see and are present at that particular place and there would not be two opinions on the subject; just as lightning is apprehended by all. Besides, if water well-established by pramāna were cognised in error, then there could not be the relation of sublator and sublated. For, all cognitions, right or wrong, would alike have well established things as their objects. Therefore, the thing which is manifested in erroneous cognition is an extra-ordinary one <p.212> (alaukika); and the theory of alaukikarthakhyati alone should be recognised, so say some (a section of the Mimamsakas).  
104 {tannirasanapūrvakaṃ smṛtipramoṣasthāpanam}

[28,4]
tad apy asāram, tasyārthasya pramāṇābhāvād asiddheḥ. yas tāvat toyādir arthaḥ pratyakṣādisiddhaḥ sa bhrāntau na pratibhāty eva, pūrvoktadoṣāt. pratyakṣādisiddhād avyatiriktas tu kutaḥ siddha iti? niṣpramāṇakatvād evālaukiko 'sāv iti cet, na, pramāṇābhāve vyavasthānupapatteḥ. anyathā hi sarvatrāpy apramāṇikaiva vyavasthā bhaviṣyatīti kiṃ pramāṇaiḥ kalpitair iti? tadarthasiddhaye tarhi viparyayākhyaṃ pramāṇaṃ kalpanīyam — na cāsāv artho 'narthakriyākārī darśanajārthakriyākāritvāt candrādivad iti. na, candrādidarśanavad avādhyatvaprasaṅgāt. na ca loke śāstre vā vādhyamānasya pramāṇatvaṃ dṛṣṭam iti. api ca yathābhūta evārthaḥ pratyakṣādibhiḥ pratīyate, tathābhūta eva bhrāntijñāne 'pi pratibhāti, nālaukiko 'nyaḥ, tenāyaṃ bhramaḥ smṛtivipramoṣa ity ucyate, dṛṣṭārthānukāritvāt tadanyasmṛtivad ity aparo manyate.		 190 The theory stated above is also not acceptable according to the 'Smrtipramosavadin (Prabhakara Mimamsaka). According to him, the notion of memory "I remember silver, and not perceive it" is obscured or lost sight of in the erroneous cognition. The theory of error known as alaukikarthakhyati is not tenable, since alaukika artha, extraordinary thing - cannot be established on the ground of any pramana. A thing like water etc. which is truly known through perception etc. is never manifest in erroneous cognition and if this were admitted, the above-mentioned fault would arise - there would be no difference between right and wrong cognitions. Moreover, it may be asked : On what ground could one establish the alaukika, extraordinary thing which is alleged to be different from the object established by perception etc. ? The answer may be that the thing manifested in erroneous cognition is alaukika only because there is no pramana for it. But this is not proper, says the proponent of the theory of error called Smṛtipramoṣa-Khyāti. For, in the absence of pramana, nothing could be established. Otherwise, things would be established everywhere without the basis of pramanas, so what is the need for the conception of pramanas ? The alaukikartha-Khyativadin may argue that if the point of showing some evidence to establish alaukika or extraordinary thing be insisted on, then let a pramana known as 'viparyaya' be conceived to establish such a thing, e.g. nacre-silver etc. Further, it is not that this alaukika thing, e.g. silver manifested in erroneous cognition, does not possess the characteristic of practical efficiency (arthakriyakaitva); since a man runs to pick it up, when he sees it at a distance. Though a man cannot reach the moon (- of course astronauts have already reached there -), yet he is provoked to do some activity by the perception of the moon. So also the nacre-silver has practical efficiency, though it cannot be actually obtained. Refuting this argl.lment the Prabhakara Mimamsaka <p.213> (who is Smrtipramoṣavadin) says that the illusory silver cannot be compared with the moon. If it be such, then there would arise the contingency of the cognition of illusory silver never being sublated. The cognition of moon is never found to be contradicted, but such is not the case with the cognition of illusory silver. Moreover, the cognition of a thing that is sublated later on by another cognition is never found to be accepted as pramana, or as valid proof either in the world (practical life) or in Sastras. Besides, we find that in illusory cognition the object appears to be, as it is apprehended in perceptual cognition and the like it is not that in illusory cognition the object appears as something alaukika - extraordinary. Hence this viparyaya, says the Prabhakara Mimamsaka, consists in the obscuration of memory - memory is taken as perceptual cognition; and not as memory. In the erroneous cognition. 'This is silver,' there are two conceptions, and not one. The conception of 'silver' is in fact memory; and as is not of the nature of apprehension, it cannot be accepted as pramana (valid); whereas the cognition of 'this' is of the nature of direct apprehension and so it is accepted as pramana. Here the conception of 'silver' which is just in the shape of memory provokes a man to take it up, but when he actually reaches, he finds that the thing he saw is not silver. Hence the cognition - 'This is silver' is erroneous.  
105 {tatkhaṇḍanam}

[28,15]
tad apy anupapannam — na hi smṛtir idaṃ rajatam ity evam ākārā bhavitum arhati, anubhavenāviśeṣaprasaṅgāt. na ca smṛtiḥ pratyayāntareṇa vādhyate, yathānubhavāveditārthaviṣayatvād iti. vipramuṣitatvād iti cet,		 191 But this theory also does not stand to reason, says the Viparitakhyativadin or anyathakhyativadin (Bhasarvajna). It may be noted that henceforth Bhasarvajna himself refutes the rival views. He says that memory cannot possibly be in the form 'This is silver'. If it were admitted as such, then there would be the contingency of memory being non-different from experience. More<p.214>over memory is not sublated by another cognition such as - 'This is not silver' because memory has its object a thing as it is presented by direct apprehension : The Prabhakara Mimamsaka may argue that illusion occurs owing to certain mental weakness which gives rise to non-discrimination between the memory of silver and the perceptual cognition of nacre. This is called obscuration of memory (Smrti-Vipramosa) and it gives rise to illusion.  
106 ko 'yaṃ vipramoṣo nāma? yadi manodoṣād anyaviṣaya utpādaḥ, sa smṛter asambhavī leśato 'pi hy apūrvāṃśapratibhāsitve pratyabhijñānavad asmṛtitvaprasaṅgaḥ syāt. yadi manodoṣād anyam eva viṣayaṃ svaviṣayākāreṇāvabhāsayati smṛtis tadā viparītakhyātir eva saṃjñāntareṇeṣṭā syāt. 192 We may ask : 'What is this obscuration'? If obscuration be regarded as the rise of memory in respect of an object other than its own cause e. g. the rise of the memory of silver in respect of nacre owing to some mental defect, that is not possible in the case of memory. For, if some new element, however little, is manifested, it would not be memory, as appears in the case of pratyabhijna (recognition). Here in the recognition there is certainly memory of the object as it was seen in the past; but it is linked to the perception of the same object in the present, and because of this new element entering, the cognition is not regarded as 'memory' , but it is regarded as 'recognition'. Here in the case of illusion there is a thing in front and it is quite distinct in form and nature from the original object of the memory. So it cannot be said that memory arises in respect of this thing, e.g. nacre and that the memory factor is obscured. If memory is said to manifest a different object in the form of its own object owing to some mental defect, then it would be equivalent to recognising Viparitakhyati only ( - which is not acceptable to the Prabhakara Mimamsaka-) by another name (viz. Smrti-Vipramoṣa).  
107 na ca manodoṣād eva bhrāntiḥ, timirādidoṣād apy utpatteḥ. ato 'pi na smṛtir iyam. athānubhavena saha kṣīrodakavad avivekena smṛter utpādo vipramoṣa ity ucyate. ko 'yam avivekaḥ kṣīrodakayor api? yadi bhinnayoḥ satorabhedena grahaṇam, tadā viparītakhyātiḥ syāt. atha saṃśleṣaviśeṣaḥ, saṃjñānayor na sambhavīti. athānantaryeṇotpādaḥ, tadānumeyaśabdārthadevadattādijñānānāṃ smaraṇānantarabhāvināṃ smṛtivipramoṣatvaprasaṅgaḥ. yad apy uktaṃ smṛtyanubhavayor vivekāgrahaṇād akhyātir eva smṛtivipramoṣaḥ. tata uttaraṃ jñānaṃ tayor bhinnaviṣayayor vivekaṃ kurvad utpadyate na bādhakaṃ kasyacid iti. tad api pratītivirodhān na yuktam. tathā hi — yad tad rajatam ity avagataṃ pūrvaṃ tacchuktikāśakalaṃ na rajatam ity evaṃ bādha utpadyamāno 'nubhūyate, na tu rajataṃ smṛtaṃ śuktikāśakalam idam anubhūtam iti. rajatam iti jñānasyedam iti jñānasamānaviṣayatvenānubhūyamānasyāpi smṛtitve śuktiketi jñānasyāpi smṛtitvaprasaṅgo, viśeṣābhāvāt. tataś cedam iti pratīyamānaṃ na rajataṃ na śuktikety anupākhyam eva prāptam. na caitad upapannam. kiṃ cātinirantarotpannāpi smṛtir anubhavabuddhiś ca yadi yathāvasthitākāreṇa svārtham avabhāsayati, tadā kutas tatra bhramavyavahāraḥ? tad anyayathāvat svārthāvabhāsijñānavat. athānyathāvabhāsayati tadā viparītakhyātir iti. 193 Moreover, it is also not correct to say that illusion arises from mental defect alone, it occurs due to the defect of partial blindness and the like also. Hence also it is not memory. Again, the Mimamsaka may argue that obscuration (Vipramosa) signifies the rise of memory ( of silver) without its discrimination from direct experience (i.e. presentation of 'this'); just as milk is not <p.215> discriminated or separated from water. Here it may be asked : What is this non-discrimination of water and milk ? If it means that both are apprehended as non-different, even though they are in fact different; then again it amounts to recognising the Viparitakhyati. According to the theory of Viparitakhyati, there is illusion, when nacre is manifested as silver, as something different from what it is. It may be urged that Smrti-pramosa is a special kind of contact between experience and memory that gives rise to error; then the reply to this is that such a contact is not possible. For, two qualities - here jnanas - (perception and memory) cannot come in contact with each other. Contact can take place only between two substances. It may be argued that smrti-Vipramosa consists in the rising of the cognition immediately after the memory of 'silver', then there would be the contingency of smrti-vipramosa in the case of all cognitions that arise immediately after memory. Knowledge of objects of inference (which occurs after the remembrance of Vyapti) as also the knowledge of the person Devadatta signified by the name 'Devadatta' occuring after the remembrance of the relation between the name and that to which the name belongs would also be Smrti-vipramosa. The Prabhakara Mimamsaka argues - Smrti- vipramosa is just akhyati (non-knowledge) because the difference between memory and experience is not grasped. The cognition that arises later ( i.e. this is not silver) arises as distinguishing between these two cognitions having their respective objects, it does not sublate anything. But this also is not proper, as it goes against our experience. It is the experience of all that erroneous cognition is sublated later when true perceptual knowledge in respect of the object arises. Man realises that the object <p.216> which was first cognised as silver was not really silver, but it was a piece of nacre. Sublation arising in this form is experienced by all. But no one realises that silver was remembered and nacre ('this') was perceived. Moreover, the cognition 'silver' is experienced as having the same object as the cognition 'this' (this is silver); and even then if it be regarded as memory, then even the cognition 'nacre' which also is experienced as having the same object as the cognition 'this' (This is nacre), should be regarded as memory, for there is no difference in the two cases. Consequently what is cognised as 'this' could not be specifically signified as 'silver' or 'nacre'. Hence it comes to be incapable of being given any name ( anupakhya). But this is not justifiable. Moreover, if the memory ( of silver) and the presentative cognition (of nacre ) though they both arising immediately one after the other without any interval whatsoever, if they illumine their own objects viz. silver and nacre respectively, as they really are, then how could it be called illusion ? This memory of silver and the perceptual cognition of nacre should be true as any other cognition which manifests its own object as it really is. If it is urged that in such a case the cognition 'this is silver' in respect of nacre manifests the object otherwise, as different from what it is - manifests nacre as silver-; then it is a case of Viparitakhyati, and not smrti-Vipramosa. Thus what is actually meant by 'Vipramosa' could not be explained by reason and hence the theory of Smrti-Vipramosa held by the Prabhakara Mimamsaka cannot be accepted.  
108 {ātmakhyātiḥ}

[29,21]
aparas tv āha — rajatajñānaṃ jalajñānam ity evamādiviśiṣṭavyavahāro viśeṣābhāve sati na yuktaḥ, tenāvaśyaṃ jñānagato 'rthagato vā viśeṣo vācyaḥ. tatra rajatāder arthasyāvidyamānatvenānupalambhād viśeṣakatvam ayuktaṃ. na hi yo yasya sambandhitvena viśeṣo nāvadhāritaḥ, sa tasya viśeṣako bhavitum arhati; smṛtyupasthāpito 'pi pūrvānubhavasyaiva viśeṣakaḥ syāt, nedānīntanasya tadasambandhitvāt. atha rajatādibhrānteḥ śuktikādir eva viṣaya iti, tad ayuktam; rajatādipratibhāsābhāvaprasaṅgāt. na hi rūpaviṣayaṃ jñānaṃ rasam avabhāsayatīti yuktam.		 194 On the other hand, another thinker, the proponent of the theory of atmakhyati, says, that in the absence of distinctive characteristic, we cannot properly speak of cognitions as distinctive, viz. 'cognition of silver,' 'cognition of water'; etc. Hence some distinctive characteristic pertaining to either the cognition or the object should necessarily be pointed out. Now, the objects, silver, and the like cannot serve as the distinguishing factors, as they, being non-existent, cannot be apprehended. That is to say, a thing like silver does not exist at all and so it cannot distinguish one cognition from another. The distinctive element which is not ascertained as being related to some particular object, can<p.217>not distinguish that particular object from others. And silver etc. being non-existent could not be shown as being related to any other things viz. cognition. The distinctive element if it be projected by memory could distinguish only the previous experience, and not the present one. That is to say, silver that we remember might serve a distinguishing factor of the previous apprehension of silver, but it cannot distinguish the present perceptual cognition 'this is silver' (actually in respect of nacre); because it is in no way related to the present cognition. It is not also correct to say that in the case of illusion of silver, the nacre itself is the object; for if it be so admitted, then there would arise the contingency of the silver and the like not being cognised at all. It is not proper that the cognition having colour for its object should illumine taste. So the erroneous cognition of silver cannot have nacre as its object.  
109 ata eva bhrāntir iti cet — atha mataṃ yata evānyad ālambanam anyac ca pratibhāti, ata eva bhrāntir ity ucyate. yasmiṃs tu jñāne tad evālambanaṃ tad eva ca pratibhāti, tattattvajñānam eveti. tad idam anupapannam; na hi pratibhāsamānārthavyatirekeṇa jñānasyālambanārtho 'nyaḥ krayakrīto 'sti. kena vā viśeṣeṇa tasyālambanatvam? yadi karmakārakatvena, tatkarmakārakatvaṃ janakatvenāviśiṣṭasya pratibhāsamānatām antareṇa katham avagantuṃ pāryate? taddeśaṃ prati varttamānatvād iti cet, na; taddeśavartinas tṛṇāder api karmakārakatvaprasaṅgāt. na hi pratibhāsam antareṇa marīcyābhimukha evāyaṃ pravṛtto na taddeśasthatṛṇādyabhimukha ity atra viśeṣahetur asti. api cātītānāgatārthapratibhāsijñānaṃ prati kasya karmakārakatvam? yadi tasyaiva, tatrāvidyamānatvāt. athānyasya, tadā tajjñānasya bhrāntatvaprasaṅgaḥ. tataś cātītādyarthapratipattyabhāva iti, tasmād yad eva pratibhāti tad evāvalambanaṃ viṣayaś cety ucyate. na ca śuktikādiviṣayaviśeṣitatve 'pi sati rajataṃ me pratibhāti, rajatajñānam idaṃ jalajñānam ity evamādivyavahāro yuktaḥ. tasmād upalambhasyaiva rajatādyākāro 'bhyupagantavyaḥ. sa cānādyavidyopaplavasāmarthyād bahir iva pratibhāti, anādivicitravāsanāś ca kramavipākavatyaḥ puṃsāṃ santi. tenānekākārāṇi vijñānāni svākāramātrasaṃvedyāni krameṇa bhavantīti tasmād ātmakhyātir eva siddheti. 195 It may be argued that this is the very reason why this is called illusion. This signifies that illusion occurs only where there is the cognition of something else, when the basis is something different. On the other hand, the cognition that manifests the thing which is its basis is true knowledge. That is to say, when the thing before us, which becomes the basis of our cognition and the thing manifested in our cognition are one and the same, it is true knowledge. So there is nothing wrong in saying that in the erroneous cognition of silver and the like, what serves as basis (object) is nacre etc and what appears is silver. But this does not stand to reason, says the proponent of the throry of atmakhyati (Vijnanavadin). For besides the object which is manifested in the cognition, there is nothing which can be purchased from the sale and could provide a basis (alambana, object). Moreover, on the strength of what speciality could it be said that the nacre is the alambana of the cognition of silver ? It may be argued that nacre is the alambana of the cognition of silver; because of the karmakarakatva. That is to say, the nacre becomes the object of the act of cognising. But this is not proper, since unless the object which is non-different from what is the generator of cognition is manifested in the cognition, how could the state of being a karmakaraka (karmakarakatva) possibly be known ? If it is argued that such a potency of karmakarakatva <p.218> could be established on the basis of the nacre being present at that particular place, then this also is not tenable. For, if this be admitted, there would arise the contigency of grass, etc. existent at that place; also having karmakarakatva. There is not any specific reason except the manifestation (of water) why a man is prompted to move towards mirage alone, and not towards the grass etc. located at that place. That is to say, cognition is the only means through which a man can apprehend an object, whatever it be, real or illusory. Hence there is no means of deciding that nacre is the alambana on account of being karmakaraka in respect of the knowledge. Moreover, one may ask : What could have the karmakarakatva or the potency of being the object of cognition manifesting past and future things ? No object of the past or of the future could be directly connected as object with the present cognition. If it is said that the very object of the past or of the future has the potency of being the object of the cognition; then it is not tenable. For, such an object does not in fact exist there. On the other hand, if some other thing is said to have this potency to become the object, then there would arise the contingency of that knowledge being erroneous. Consequently there would not be the cognition of the things of the past etc. Hence whatever becomes manifest is itself the alambana (basis) and the Visaya (object) of that cognition. It is not proper if one says, 'I cognise silver', 'this is the cognition of silver,' 'this is the cognition of water' or the like; even though the cognition is actually characterised by an object which is nacre or the like. Therefore, it should be accepted that it is cognition itself that assumes the shape of silver etc. Such a shape of silver etc. assumed by the cognition is in fact internal, but it appears as if external on account of being overpowered by the beginningless avidya (nescience) which upsets things. And people do have such impressions (vasanas) which are beginningless and diverse in nature, and which fructify in succession. Hence the cognitions having diverse shapes and having their own shapes alone as their knowable objects arise in succession. Therefore, the theory of error called atmakhyati alone, in cognition its own self or shape is cognised, <p.219> proves to be acceptable. The Vijnanavadin Buddhist believes that even the so-called real objects like silver etc. are illusion from the view-point of ultimate reality, this being the principal illusion; while when we mistake nacre for silver it is what we may call the ephemeral illusion.  
110 {ātmakhyātipratyākhyānam}

[30,24]
naitad api yuktam, svātmasaṃvittiniṣṭhatve arthākāratve ca hi jñānasyātmakhyātiḥ sidhyeta. tad ubhayam apy uttaratra nirākariṣyāmaḥ. kiṃ ca sarvajñānānāṃ svātmākāragrāhitvāviśeṣe bhrāntābhrāntaviveko vādhyavādhakabhāvaś ca na prāpnoti, svātmākāravyabhicārābhāvād iti. yadi ca jñānātmani sthita eva rajatādyākāraḥ saṃvedyate, tadā sukhādyākāravan na bahiṣṭhaḥ pratīyeta. pratipattāpi tadupādānārthaṃ na pravarteta, asthiratvena pravṛttyaviṣayatvād iti. athāvidyopaplavavaśād vahiṣṭhasthiratvādhyavasāya iti manyase. nanv evaṃ tarhi viparītakhyātir eveṣṭā bhavati. bodhābhinnasyāsthirasyārthākārasyānyathādhyavasāyābhyupagamād iti.		 196 The theory of atmakhyati also is not justifiable, says Bhasarvajna. For, it could be established only when knowledge is proved to be based on the cognition of its own self and to be taking the shapes of objects. Bhasarvajna refutes both these views later on. Further, if all cognitions are said to be alike grasping the shapes of their own self, then it would not be possible to distinguish between illusory cognition and non-illusory cognition and there would not be the relation of sublator and sublated; since there is no scope for discrepancy of knowledge in regard to the shape of its own self. And if the shape of silver etc. present only in the nature of knowledge is cognised, then silver etc. would not appear as external like the shape of pleasure etc. (which we experience as internal). And the person who sees that illusory silver (which should be known as internal only) would not proceed to pick it up; since the silver etc. being evanescent (as the consciousness is regarded as momentary by the Vijnanavadin) could not be made the object of any activity whatsoever. If it is argued that silver <p.220> etc. are in fact internal and evanescent in nature but they seem to be reverse, viz. external and enduring owing to the efficacy of Avidya (nescience); then it comes to the acceptance of the theory of Viparitakhyati. For, according to this theory a thing is wrongly determined to be what it is really not. In the present instance, the shape of silver which is non-different from its cognition and which is not enduring is wrongly determined as otherwise, viz. different from its cognition and as enduring. Therefore this is what we call Viparitakhyati.  
111 {anirvacanīyakhyātiḥ}

[31,8]
anyas tv āha — na jñānasya viṣaya upadeśagamyo 'numānasādhyo vā, yena viparīto 'rthaḥ kalpyeta. kiṃ tarhi? yo yasmin jñāne ca bhāti, sa tasya viṣaya ity ucyate. jalādijñāne ca jalādyartha eva pratibhāti, na tadviparītaḥ, jalādijñānavyapadeśābhāvaprasaṅgāt. sa jalādir arthaḥ san na bhavati, tadbuddher abhrāntatvaprasaṅgāt. nāpy asan, khapuṣpādivat pratibhāsapravṛttyor aviṣayatvaprasaṅgāt. nāpi sadasadātmakaḥ, ubhayadoṣāt, sadasator aikātmyavirodhāc ca. digambaramatenāpi sarvajñānānāṃ sadasadātmakārthapratibhāsitvāviśeṣād bhrāntābhrāntavyavasthānupapattiḥ, tasmād ayaṃ buddhisaṃdarśito 'rthaḥ sattvenāsattvenānyena vā dharmāntareṇa nirvaktuṃ na śakyata iti. ata evāvidyā brahmaṇaḥ kiṃ bhinnāthābhinnetyādivikalpo niravakāśaḥ. kasmāt na hy avidyā paramārthataḥ kenāpi rūpeṇa nirvacanīyā. yadi nirvacanīyā syāt, tatas tattvam eva syāt nāvidyeti. atattvarūpā hy avidyā sarvair iṣṭā tasmād anirvacanīyārthakhyātiḥ siddheti.		 197 Still another thinker, who advocates the theory of anirvacaniya-Khyati urges as follws : The object of knowledge is not something which can be known by the instruction (verbal testimony) of another, or which can be established by inference, so that a reverse object could be imagined. That is to say, rajju (rope) cannot be cognised to be the object of Sarpajnana (knowledge of serpent). What is it then? That which appears or is manifest in a particular cognition is said to be its object. And in the cognition of water, what is manifested is water alone, and nothing else which is reverse ( i.e. different from it). If something other than water, etc; e. g. rays of the sun etc. were manifested in the cognition of water, then there would be the contingency of its not being designated as the cognition of water; for things are named as they are known. However, that object, water or the like, which appears in an illusory cognition is not-existent (sat), for otherwise there would arise the contingency of that cognition being non-erroneous (- which it is not). Nor is that object, water or the like, which appears in the erroneous cognition non-existent (asat), for, in that case also there would arise the contingency that such a (non-existent) thing like the sky-flower could not become the object of the cognition or of <p.221>the activity of moving towards it. Nor also is that object which appears in the illusory cognition, existent and non-existent simultaneously (sad-asadatmaka) for in that case, both the above-mentioned faults would be there and besides this there would be the contradiction in regarding existent and non-existent as being of a unitary nature. According to the view of Digambara Jaina also all cognitions alike manifest an object of the nature of existence and non-existence; and consequently there would not be any justification for the classification of cognitions as erroneous and non-erroneous. This leads us to conclude that the object presented by consciousness cannot be specifically described as existent or non-existent or as having any other characteristic. This is the very reason why there is no scope for indulging in dialectical discussions such as - 'Is avidya (nescience) different from Brahman or non-different from it ?' Indeed, from the view-point of ultimate reality avidya cannot be specifically described in any form. If it could have been so described, it would have been a reality, and not avidya (nescience). But all recognise that avidya is of the nature of non-reality (atattvarupa). Therefore, it is concluded that the theory of anirvacaniya-khyati is the only theory that can explain the phenomenon of illusion.   
112 {tatpratyākhyānam anyathākhyātiprakhyāpanaṃ ca}

[31,21]
etad apy ayuktam — advaitasiddhau tatsidhyati, tac cādvaitaṃ nirākariṣyāmaḥ. yac coktaṃ na vijñānasya viṣaya upadeśagamya ityādi, nanu bhavatām evopadeśagamyaḥ prāptaḥ. tathā hi — jalādibhrāntau niyatadeśakālasvabhāvaḥ sadātmakatvenaiva jalādir arthaḥ pratibhāti, tadgrahaṇepsos tatraiva pravṛttidarśanāt. katham asāv anirvacanīyaḥ syāt? na hy evaṃ bhūte pratibhāsapravṛttī aniracanīye 'rthe sambhavata iti. atha vicāryamāṇa evāsau sadasattvādibhir anirvacanīyaḥ saṃpadyate, na tu bhrāntikāle tathā pratibhātīti. evaṃ tarhi anyathāpratibhāsād viparītakhyātir eveti. nanu ca viparītakhyātir api pratibhāsavirodhān na yukteti, na, abhiprāyāparijñānāt. na brūmo viparīto 'yam artha ity evaṃ khyātiḥ, kiṃ tarhi? puruṣaviparīte sthāṇau puruṣo 'yam iti khyātir viparītakhyātir ity ucyate. nanu ca puruṣāvabhāsini jñāne sthāṇor apratibhāsamānasya viṣayatvam ayuktam, sarvatrāpy avyavasthāprasaṅgād iti. naitad asti sthāṇur eva hi puruṣo 'yam ity etasmin jñāne pratibhāti, na puruṣaḥ, puruṣāvabhāsijñānam ity etad ity ayam api bhramaḥ sthāṇupratibhāsitvāt. tathā hi — bādhottarakālaṃ pratisaṃdhatte — sthāṇur ayaṃ me puruṣa ity evaṃ pratibhāsa iti. dravyabuddhyā ca keṣāṃcid apadravyagrahaṇaṃ dṛṣṭam, tad api viparītakhyātāv eva yujyate. tathā ca hastena gṛhītvānyeṣām api kathayati — mayedaṃ suvarṇaṃ rajataṃ vā labdhaṃ krītaṃ veti. tasmāt sthāṇvādir eva tattvasyāgrahaṇād adharmādivaśāc ca puruṣādyākāreṇādhyavasīyata iti yuktam.		 198 At this stage, Bhasarvajna of the Nyaya school proceeds to repudiate the theory of anirvacaiya-Khyati and to establish his own theory of anyathakhyati. He says that this anirvacaniyakhyati also is not tenable. For, this view could be acceptable, if non-duality could be established. Bhasarvajna refutes the theory of <p.222>non-duality later on in the third pariccheda of the Nyayabhusana. There he quotes certain sentences from the Upanisads which clearly support the theory of duality. Moreover, if the theory of non-duality be accepted, how could it be established ? For, pramanas also being based on difference. arc illusory. If such pramanas could establish non-duality then they could establish duality also. 'If the knowledge of non-duality is said to be different from illusory cognition, then the difference is bound to come in. Again, if there is no difference, how could one distinguisb pratijiia from hetu ? All this shows that the theory of non-duality cannot be maintained. And as said above, unless non-duality is established, the theory of anirvacaniyakhyati cannot be accepted. Moreover, as to what is said by the Advaitavadin that the object of erroneous cognition is not to be known by the instructior or anybody, and so on; Bhasarvajna says that it is the Advaitavadin himself who made that object known by his instruction ( - according to his instruction the object of erroneous cognition is anirvacaniya.) To wit in the illusory cognition of water or the like, an object, water or the like having a definite place and time and nature is manifested as existent (sat) only (and not as anirvacaniya), because one who wants to attain it, is seen to be active in that direction. Then how could such an object be indescribable (anirvacaniya)? Indeed in regard to such an indescribable object, cognition and the activity provoked by it could not be possible. It may be argued that on critical examination this thing becomes (turns out to be) indescribable as existent, non-existent etc; but it does not appear as such at the time of the illusion. The answer to this is that in that case since it appears as different from what it really is, it is a case of Viparitakhyati (and not anirvacaniyakhyati). If it is urged that the Viparitakhyati also is not proper, since it is <p.223> contradicted by the cognition (viz. even in the erroneous cognition, the thing is manifested as silver, water, etc; and not as reverse of something); the answer is that it is not so, for, the contradiction is pointed out because the opponent has not caught the point. It is not meant to be conveyed that Viparitakhyati signifies the knowledge of the form-'This object is reverse' (i. e,) different from what it really is). What viparitakhyati signifies is the knowledge viz. 'it is a man' in respect of a pillar, which is reverse of a man ( i.e. different in character from a man). Again, it may be urged that it is not proper to say that a pillar which is not manifested in the cognition at all is the object of the cognition which manifests a man. And if this be admitted, then there would arise the contingency of everything being in disorder - that is, any knowledge could have just anything as its object. The answer to this is that it is not so; as a matter of fact it is the post that is actually cognised in the cognition 'This is a man', and not the man. To regard it as knowledge manifesting a man is also error, as it actually manifests a post. To wit, after the rise of the sublative cognition the person links up his cognition thus : 'This post appeared to me as man'. Moreover, the fact that some mistake fake substances as genuine can also be explained only by the theory of Viparitakhyati. And persons are so much illusioned that they take up such fake substances in the hand and tell others : 'I have found or purchased this silver or gold'. Therefore, it is proper to conclude that post or the like is determined as man or the like in an erroneous cognition due to the non-apprehension <p.224> of its real nature and due to demerit etc. Therefore, Viparitakhyati is the only theory which can be acceptable.  
113 {svapnārthavimarśaḥ}

[32,15]
atha svapnādijñāne kas tarhi viparīto 'rtha iti? taddeśasthaḥ khaṭvādir ity eke. tan na; pramāṇābhāvāt. na hi sannihitatvād eva jñānasya viṣayaḥ, kiṃ tarhi? yaḥ pratibhāti sa cottarakālaṃ pratisaṃdhānabalenāpi vyavasthāpyate. na ca khaṭvādau pratisaṃdhānam apy asti, tasmān nāsau viṣayaḥ, kiṃ tarhi? pūrvopalabdho 'nupalabdho vā vidyamāno 'vidyamāno vā yaḥ pratibuddhāvasthāyāṃ bādhakapratyayena sthāṇvādivad anusaṃdhīyate, sa khalu deśakālasvabhāvānyatvena svapnajñāne pratibhāsanāt viparīta ity ucyate. tathā hi — prabuddhaḥ san kathayati — mayādya svapne mṛtaḥ pitā jīvatīti dṛṣṭaḥ, putras tu deśāntarasthita ihasthita iti, andho nāndha ity evamādi. keśoṇḍukajñāne 'py avidyamānaḥ keśasamūhaḥ sadākāratayā pratibhāti, bādhakotpattau tathānusaṃdhānāt. nanu cāvidyamānasyālambanatvāyogān nirviṣayo viparyayaḥ prāpta iti. na, atītānāgatayor avidyamānatve 'py ālambanatvāt. nirālambanatve hy akhyātir eva syāt. tatra ca doṣa ukta iti. atītānāgatasvapnādijñānānām avidyamānārthālambanatvāviśeṣāt, bhrāntābhrāntaviśeṣānupapattir iti cet, na; tadrūpātadrūpagṛhītatvena tattvajñānetarayor viśeṣotpatteḥ. na hi vidyamānāvidyamānārthālambanatvena tattvajñānetaravibhāgaḥ, kiṃ tarhi? avidyamāno 'py artho yadā tathābhūtenaivāvidyamānākāreṇa niścīyate, tadā tadviṣayaṃ tattvajñānam eva; tadrūpāvyabhicārāt. yadā tu vidyamānenānyena vā viparītākāreṇāsann artho 'dhyavasīyate, tadā bhrāntir iti.		 199 Then, Bhasarvajna proceeds to explain the nature of illusion with special reference to dream. It may be asked: What is the Viparita (reverse) object in the case of dream-knowledge or the like ? Some say that it is the bedstead (khatva) or the like that is there at that place. But it is not so, since there is no proof for this. It is not proper to say that a thing becomes an object of knowledge only because it is near at hand. In fact, what is manifested as object in the dream-cognition or the like is later adjudged as such (i.e. as object) even on the strength of the knowledge that serves to link up the earlier cognition. And there is no such pratisandhana (linking up) in regard to the bedstead or the like. So the bedstead is not the object of the dream-cognition. Whatever be the object previously apprehended or not apprehended existent or non-existent, that is linked up or recollected by the sublating cognition in the waking state, that is the object; - just as when a person links up the post as the object of erroneous cognition (I was cognising the post as man), when the person apprehends the post as it really is; that same thing is manifested in the dream-cognition as associated with a different place, time or nature and so is said to be 'viparita' (reverse). To wit, on waking a person says, 'I saw my dead father as living (- associated with different time - Kalanyathatva); I saw my son who is in a foreign country as present here (- different place - desanyathatva); a blind man as not blind ( - different nature Svabhavanythatva). Even in the case of the illusory cognition of hair-like apparitions, the non-existent mass of hair appears as having the form of 'existent’. For, when it is sublated later, the person realises that the non-existent mass of hair was cognised by him as existent. Thus the object of erroneous cognition is found recollected in the later knowledge. It may be contended that in view of the above examples, Viparyaya or illusion comes to be devoid of any object, since a non-existent <p.225> thing cannot properly be an alambana (basis) of cognition. The answer to this is that the contention is not tenable. For, the things of the past and of the future, though being non-existent at present, do serve as alambana. And if there be no alambana, it would be a case of akhyati, which is already repudiated. Again, an objection may be raised that thus it would not be possible to differentiate illusory cognitions from non-illusory ones, because the cognitions of past and future objects and so also dream-cognitions have all alike non-existent things as their objects. Yet how is it that we regard dream-cognitions as erroneous, and cognitions of past and future objects as non-erroneous ? The answer to this is : It is not so; for, it is justifiable to distinguish true cognitions from illusory ones, inasmuch as the former grasp things as they really are, while the latter grasp things as they are not. It is not that right cognition and erroneous cognition are differentiated on the basis of their having existent and non-existent things respectively as their objects. A thing may be non-existent, but if it is ascertained just as having a non-existent shape (i. e. as non-existent) then the knowledge having that as its object is certainly true knowledge; for it does not deviate from its form. But if a non-existent object is ascertained as existent or as having any reverse character (i.e. character different from what it really has), then the knowledge is erroneous, So if (existent) water manifested in perceptual cognition is ascertained as it really is, then it is a non-erroneous cognition of water; but if non-existent water is ascertained as real water, then it is an erroneous cognition.  
114 [33,8]
etena yad āhur eke — satyodakam asatyodakaṃ cety udakaprapañco 'yaṃ madhurodakādivat. tatra yathā satyodakabuddhau nāsatyodakaṃ pratibhāti, anudakaṃ vā, evam asatyodakajñāne 'pi na satyodakaṃ pratibhāty anudakaṃ veti. svaviṣaye paryavasāyinyo hi buddhayaḥ. tasmān na viparīto 'stīti. tad api nirastam, na hy asatyodakaṃ nāma kiṃcid asti. vidyamānam api yadīdam asatyodakam ity evaṃ niścīyate, tadā bhaved abhrāntajñānam. yadā tu pānādisamarthodakākāreṇādhyavasīyate, tadā katham abhrāntajñānam? viparītārthādhyavasāyasya sarvalokaśāstreṣu bhrāntatvena prasiddhatvāt. tadvirodhena ca pravarttamāno 'yaṃ na laukiko na parīkṣaka ity upekṣaṇīya eveti.		 200 Here Bhasarvajna refers to a view held by some, according to which real water, non-real water etc. are diverse phenomenal <p.226> forms of water, like sweet water etc. Moreover, according to this view, just as in the cognition of real water, illusory water or non-water is not manifested, so in the cognition of illusory water, real water or non-water is not manifested. Indeed all cognitions result in the manifestation of their own objects. Hence, according to this view, there is no cognition in which an object could be said to be viparita (i.e. other than what it really is). This view also, says Bhasarvajna, stands repudiated by the above arguments adduced in the discussion of dreams. As a matter of fact, a thing like non-real water does not exist at all. And even if a thing like non-real water is admitted as something that is there, and if it is ascertained in the cognition as non-real water, then that cognition is certainly non-erroneous, inasmuch as it manifests the thing as it really is. But when it ( i.e. non-real water) is ascertained as of the form of water, which is capable of being drunk etc; how could that cognition be non-erroneous ? It is certainly erroneous, as it manifests a thing as what it really is not. And it is well-known everywhere in Sastras as well as in the world that a determination in respect of a 'Viparita' (reverse) object is an erroneous one. Whosoever proceeds against this well-established fact is neither an ordinary man nor a critical investigator, and so he deserves to be ignored. Thus Bhasarvajna refutes all the other theories of error, akhyati, asatkhyati etc. and establishes the theory of error called Viparitakhyati, which is acceptable to the Nyaya school.
